            "content": "2. <b>Varс╣Зa</b>┬ж (lit. тАШcolourтАЩ) in the Rigveda<sup>1</sup> is applied to denote classes of men, the <b>D─Бsa</b> and the ─Аryan Varс╣Зa being contrasted, as other passages<sup>2</sup> show, on account of colour. But this use is confined to distinguishing two colours: in this respect the Rigveda differs fundamentally from the later Saс╣Гhit─Бs and Br─Бhmaс╣Зas,<sup>3</sup> where the four castes (<i>varс╣З─Бс╕е</i>) are already fully recognized. <dvsn n='P'/><i>(a) Caste in the Rigveda.</i> тАФ The use of the term Varс╣Зa is not, of course, conclusive for the question whether caste existed in the Rigveda. In one sense it must be admitted to have existed: the Puruс╣гa-s┼лkta, тАШhymn of man,тАЩ in the tenth Maс╣Зс╕Нala<sup>4</sup> clearly contemplates the division of mankind into four classes тАФ the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, R─Бjanya, Vai┼Ыya, and ┼Ъ┼лdra. But the hymn being admittedly late,<sup>5</sup> its evidence is not cogent for the bulk of the Rigveda. Zimmer<sup>6</sup> has with great force combatted the view that the Rigveda was produced in a society that knew the caste system. He points out that the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas<sup>7</sup> show us the Vedic Indians on the Indus as unbrahminized, and not under the caste system; he argues that the Rigveda was the product of tribes living in the Indus region and the Panjab; later on a part of this people, who had wandered farther east, developed the peculiar civilization of the caste system. He adopts the arguments of Muir,<sup>8</sup> derived from the study of the data of the Rigveda, viz.: that (<i>a</i>) the four castes appear only in the late Puruс╣гas┼лkta; (<i>b</i>) the term Varс╣Зa, as shown above, covers the three highest castes of later times, and is only contrasted with D─Бsa; (<i>c</i>) that Br─Бhmaс╣Зa is rare in the Rigveda, Kс╣гatriya occurs seldom,<sup>9</sup> R─Бjanya only in the Puruс╣гas┼лkta, where too, alone, Vai┼Ыya and ┼Ъ┼лdra are found; (<i>d</i>) that Brahman denotes at first тАШpoet,тАЩ тАШsage,тАЩ and then тАШofficiating priest,тАЩ or still later a special class of priest; (<i>e</i>) that in some only of the passages<sup>10</sup> where it occurs does Brahman denote a тАШpriest by profession,тАЩ while in others it denotes something peculiar to the individual, designating a person distinguished for genius or virtue, or specially chosen to receive divine inspiration.<sup>11</sup> Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, on the other hand, as Muir admits,<sup>12</sup> already denotes a hereditary professional priesthood. <dvsn n='P'/>Zimmer connects the change from the casteless system of the Rigveda to the elaborate system of the Yajurveda with the advance of the Vedic Indians to the east, comparing the Germanic invasions that transformed the German tribes into monarchies closely allied with the church. The needs of a conquering people evoke the monarch; the lesser princes sink to the position of nobles; for repelling the attacks of aborigines or of other ─Аryan tribes, and for quelling the revolts of the subdued population, the state requires a standing army in the shape of the armed retainers of the king, and beside the nobility of the lesser princes arises that of the king's chief retainers, as the Thegns supplemented the Gesiths of the Anglo-Saxon monarchies.<sup>13</sup> At the same time the people ceased to take part in military matters, and under climatic influences left the conduct of war to the nobility and their retainers, devoting themselves to agriculture, pastoral pursuits, and trade. But the advantage won by the nobles over the people was shared by them with the priesthood, the origin of whose power lies in the Purohitaship, as Roth first saw.<sup>14</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>Originally the prince could sacrifice for himself and the people, but the Rigveda<sup>15</sup> itself shows cases, like those of <b>Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra</b> and <b>Vasiс╣гс╣нha</b> illustrating forcibly the power of the Purohita, though at the same time the right of the noble to act as Purohita is seen in the case of <b>Dev─Бpi ─Аrс╣гс╣нiс╣гeс╣Зa.</b><sup>16</sup> The Brahmins saw their opportunity, through the Purohitaship, of gaining practical power during the confusion and difficulties of the wars of invasion, and secured it, though only after many struggles, the traces of which are seen in the Epic tradition.<sup>17</sup> The Atharvaveda<sup>18</sup> also preserves relics of these conflicts in its narration of the ruin of the <b>Sс╣Ы├▒jayas</b> because of oppressing Brahmins, and besides other hymns of the Atharvaveda (viiixii), the ┼Ъatarudriya litany of the Yajurveda<sup>19</sup> reflects the period of storm and stress when the aboriginal population was still seething with discontent, and Rudra was worshipped as the patron god of all sorts of evil doers.<sup>20</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>This version of the development of caste has received a good deal of acceptance in its main outlines, and it may almost be regarded as the recognized version.<sup>21</sup> It has, however, always been opposed by some scholars, such as Haug,<sup>22</sup> Kern,<sup>23</sup> Ludwig,<sup>24</sup> and more recently by Oldenberg<sup>25</sup> and by Geldner.<sup>26</sup> The matter may be to some extent simplified by recognizing at once that the caste system is one that has progressively developed, and that it is not legitimate to see in the Rigveda the full caste system even of the Yajurveda; but at the same time it is difficult to doubt that the system was already well on its way to general acceptance. The argument from the nonbrahminical character of the <b>Vr─Бtyas</b> of the Indus and Panjab loses its force when it is remembered that there is much evidence in favour of placing the composition of the bulk of the Rigveda, especially the books<sup>27</sup> in which <b>Sud─Бs</b> appears with Vasiс╣гс╣нha and Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra, in the east, the later <b>Madhyade┼Ыa,</b> a view supported by Pischel,<sup>28</sup> Geldner,<sup>29</sup> Hopkins,<sup>30</sup> and Macdonell.<sup>31</sup> Nor is it possible to maintain that Brahman in the Rigveda merely means a тАШpoetтАЩ or тАШsage.тАЩ It is admitted by Muir that in some passages it must mean a hereditary profession; in fact, there is not a single passage in which it occurs where the sense of тАШpriestтАЩ is not allowable, since the priest was of course the singer. Moreover, there are traces in the Rigveda of the threefold<sup>32</sup> or fourfold<sup>33</sup> division of the people into <i>brahma, kс╣гatram,</i> and <i>vi┼Ыaс╕е,</i> or into the three classes and the servile population. Nor even in respect to the later period, any more than to the Rigveda, is the view correct that regards the Vai┼Ыyas as not taking part in war. The Rigveda evidently<sup>34</sup> knows of no restriction of war to a nobility and its retainers, but the late Atharvaveda<sup>35</sup> equally classes the folk with the <i>bala,</i> тАШpower,тАЩ representing the Vi┼Ы as associated with the <b>Sabh─Б,</b> <b>Samiti,</b> and <b>Sen─Б,</b> the assemblies of the people and the armed host. Zimmer<sup>36</sup> explains these references as due to tradition only; but this is hardly a legitimate argument, resting, as it does, on the false assumption that only a Kс╣гatriya can fight. But it is (see <b>Kс╣гatriya</b>) very doubtful whether Kс╣гatriya means anything more than a member of the nobility, though later, in the Epic, it included the retainers of the nobility, who increased in numbers with the growth of military monarchies, and though later the ordinary people did not necessarily take part in wars, an abstention that is, however, much exaggerated if it is treated as an absolute one. The Kс╣гatriyas were no doubt a hereditary body; monarchy was already hereditary (see <b>R─Бjan</b>), and it is admitted that the ┼Ъ┼лdras were a separate body: thus all the elements of the caste system were already in existence. The Purohita, indeed, was a person of great importance, but it is clear, as Oldenberg<sup>37</sup> urges, that he was not the creator of the power of the priesthood, but owed his position, and the influence he could in consequence exert, to the fact that the sacrifice required for its proper performance the aid of a hereditary priest in whose possession was the traditional sacred knowledge. <dvsn n='P'/>Nor can any argument for the non-existence of the caste system be derived from cases like that of <b>Dev─Бpi.</b> For, in the first place, the Upaniс╣гads show kings in the exercise of the priestly functions of learning and teaching, and the Upaniс╣гads are certainly contemporaneous with an elaborated caste system. In the second place the Rigvedic evidence is very weak, for Dev─Бpi, who certainly acts as Purohita, is not stated in the Rigveda to be a prince at all, though Y─Бska<sup>38</sup> calls him a Kauravya; the hymns attributed to kings and others cannot be vindicated for them by certain evidence, though here, again, the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas do not scruple to recognize R─Бjanyarс╣гis, or тАШroyal sagesтАЩ; and the famous Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra shows in the Rigveda no sign of the royal character which the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas insist on fastening on him in the shape of royal descent in the line of <b>Jahnu.</b><sup>39</sup> <dvsn n='P'/><i>(b) Caste in the later Saс╣Гhit─Бs and Br─Бhmaс╣Зas.</i> тАФ The relation between the later and the earlier periods of the Vedic history of caste must probably be regarded in the main as the hardening of a system already formed by the time of the Rigveda. <dvsn n='P'/>1. <i>The Names of the Castes.</i> тАФ The most regular names are Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, R─Бjanya, Vai┼Ыya, and ┼Ъ┼лdra,<sup>40</sup> or later Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, Kс╣гatriya, Vai┼Ыya, and ┼Ъ┼лdra.<sup>41</sup> There are many other variants: Brahman, Kс╣гatra, ┼Ъ┼лdr─Бryau;<sup>42</sup> Brahman, R─Бjanya, ┼Ъ┼лdra, ─Аrya;<sup>43</sup> Brahman, R─Бjanya, Vai┼Ыya, ┼Ъ┼лdra;<sup>44</sup> Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, R─Бjan, Vi┼Ыya, ┼Ъ┼лdra;<sup>45</sup> Deva, R─Бjan, ┼Ъ┼лdra, ─Аrya;<sup>46</sup> and Brahman, Kс╣гatra, Vi┼Ы, and ┼Ъ┼лdra.<sup>47</sup> In other cases the fourth class is represented by a special member: Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, Kс╣гatriya, Vai┼Ыya, and C─Бс╣Зс╕Н─Бla.<sup>48</sup> Often only the three upper classes are mentioned, as Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, R─Бjanya, Vai┼Ыya;<sup>49</sup> Brahman, Kс╣гatram, Vi┼Ы,<sup>50</sup> etc.<sup>51</sup> Three castes тАФ Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, R─Бjan, ┼Ъ┼лdra тАФ are mentioned in the Atharvaveda,<sup>52</sup> and two castes are repeatedly mentioned together, either Brahman and Kс╣гatra, or Kс╣гatra and Vi┼Ы.<sup>53</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>2. <i>The Relation of the Castes.</i> тАФ The ritual literature is full of minute differences respecting the castes. Thus, for example, the ┼Ъatapatha prescribes different sizes of funeral mounds for the four castes,<sup>54</sup> Different modes of address are laid down for the four castes,<sup>55</sup> as <i>ehi,</i> тАШapproachтАЩ; <i>─Бgaccha,</i> тАШcomeтАЩ; <i>─Бdrava,</i> тАШrun upтАЩ; <i>─Бdh─Бva,</i> тАШhasten up,тАЩ which differ in degrees of politeness. The representatives of the four castes are dedicated at the Puruс╣гamedha (тАШhuman sacrificeтАЩ) to different deities.<sup>56</sup> The S┼лtras have many similar rules.<sup>57</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>But the three upper castes in some respects differ markedly from the fourth, the ┼Ъ┼лdras. The latter are in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>58</sup> declared not fit to be addressed by a D─лkс╣гita, тАШconsecrated person,тАЩ and no ┼Ъ┼лdra is to milk the cow whose milk is to be used for the Agnihotra<sup>59</sup> (тАШfire-oblationтАЩ). On the other hand, in certain passages, the ┼Ъ┼лdra is given a place in the Soma sacrifice,<sup>60</sup> and in the Taittir─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>61</sup> there are given formul├ж for the placing of the sacrificial fire not only for the three upper castes, but also for the <b>Rathak─Бra,</b> тАШchariot-maker.тАЩ Again, in the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>62</sup> the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa is opposed as тАШeater of the oblationтАЩ to the members of the other three castes. <dvsn n='P'/>The characteristics of the several castes are given under <b>Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, Kс╣гatriya</b> and <b>R─Бjan, Vai┼Ыya, ┼Ъ┼лdra:</b> they may be briefly summed up as follows: The Vi┼Ы forms the basis of the state on which the Brahman and Kс╣гatra rest;<sup>63</sup> the Brahman and Kс╣гatra are superior to the Vi┼Ы;<sup>64</sup> while all three classes are superior to the ┼Ъ┼лdras. The real power of the state rested with the king and his nobles, with their retainers, who may be deemed the Kс╣гatriya element. Engaged in the business of the protection of the country, its administration, the decision of legal cases, and in war, the nobles subsisted, no doubt, on the revenues in kind levied from the people, the king granting to them villages (see <b>Gr─Бma</b>) for their maintenance, while some of them, no doubt, had lands of their own cultivated for them by slaves or by tenants. The states were seemingly small:<sup>65</sup> there are no clear signs of any really large kingdoms, despite the mention of <b>Mah─Бr─Бjas.</b> The people, engaged in agriculture, pastoral pursuits, and trade (<b>Vaс╣Зij</b>), paid tribute to the king and nobles for the protection afforded them. That, as BadenPowell suggests,<sup>66</sup> they were not themselves agriculturists is probably erroneous; some might be landowners on a large scale, and draw their revenues from ┼Ъ┼лdra tenants, or even ─Аryan tenants, but that the people as a whole were in this position is extremely unlikely.<sup>67</sup> In war the people shared the conflicts of the nobles, for there was not yet any absolute separation of the functions of the several classes. The priests may be divided into two classes тАФ the Purohitas of the kings, who guided their employers by their counsel, and were in a position to acquire great influence in the state, as it is evident they actually did, and the ordinary priests who led quiet lives, except when they were engaged on some great festival of a king or a wealthy noble.<sup>68</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>The relations and functions of the castes are well summed up in a passage of the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>69</sup> which treats of them as opposed to the Kс╣гatriya. The Br─Бhmaс╣Зa is a receiver of gifts (<i>─Б-d─Бy─л</i>), a drinker of Soma (<i>─Б-p─Бy─л</i>), a seeker of food (<i>─Бvas─Бy─л</i>),<sup>70</sup> and liable to removal at will (<i>yath─Бk─Бma-pray─Бpyaс╕е</i>).<sup>71</sup> The Vai┼Ыya is tributary to another (<i>anyasya balikс╣Ыt</i>), to be lived on by another (<i>anyasy─Бdyaс╕е</i>), and to be oppressed at will (<i>yath─Б-</i> <i>k─Бma-jyeyaс╕е</i>).<sup>72</sup> The ┼Ъ┼лdra is the servant of another (<i>anyasya</i> <i>preс╣гyaс╕е</i>), to be expelled at will (<i>k─Бmotth─Бpyaс╕е</i>), and to be slain at pleasure (<i>yath─Бk─Бma-vadhyaс╕е</i>).<sup>73</sup> The descriptions seem calculated to show the relation of each of the castes to the R─Бjanya. Even the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa he can control, whilst the Vai┼Ыya is his inferior and tributary, whom he can remove without cause from his land,<sup>74</sup> but who is still free, and whom he cannot maim or slay without due process. The ┼Ъ┼лdra has no rights of property or life against the noble, especially the king. <dvsn n='P'/>The passage is a late one, and the high place of the Kс╣гatriya is to some extent accounted for by this fact. It is clear that in the course of time the Vai┼Ыya fell more and more in position with the hardening of the divisions of caste. Weber<sup>75</sup> shows reason for believing that the V─Бjapeya sacrifice, a festival of which a chariot race forms an integral part,<sup>76</sup> was, as the ┼Ъ─Бс╣Еkh─Бyana ┼Ъrauta S┼лtra<sup>77</sup> says, once a sacrifice for a Vai┼Ыya, as well as for a priest or king. But the king, too, had to suffer diminution of his influence at the hands of the priest: the Taittir─лya texts<sup>78</sup> show that the V─Бjapeya was originally a lesser sacrifice which, in the case of a king, was followed by the R─Бjas┼лya, or consecration of him as an overlord of lesser kings, and in that of the Brahmin by the Bс╣Ыhaspatisava, a festival celebrated on his appointment as a royal Purohita. But the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>79</sup> exalts the V─Бjapeya, in which a priest could be the sacrificer, over the R─Бjas┼лya, from which he was excluded, and identifies it with the Bс╣Ыhaspatisava, a clear piece of juggling in the interests of the priestly pretentions. But we must not overestimate the value of such passages, or the exaltation of the Purohita in the later books of the ┼Ъatapatha and Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зas as evidence of a real growth in the priestly power: these books represent the views of the priests of what their own powers should be, and to some extent were in the Madhyade┼Ыa. Another side of the picture is presented in the P─Бli literature,<sup>80</sup> which, belonging to a later period than the Vedic, undoubtedly underestimates the position of the priests; while the Epic,<sup>81</sup> more nearly contemporaneous with the later Vedic period, displays, despite all priestly redaction, the temporal superiority of the nobility in clear light. <dvsn n='P'/>Although clear distinctions were made between the different castes, there is little trace in Vedic literature of one of the leading characteristics of the later system, the impurity communicated by the touch or contact of the inferior castes,<sup>82</sup> which is seen both directly in the purification rendered necessary in case of contact with a ┼Ъ┼лdra, and indirectly in the prohibition of eating in company with men of lower caste.<sup>83</sup> It is true that prohibition of eating in company with others does appear,<sup>84</sup> but not in connexion with caste: its purpose is to preserve the peculiar sanctity of those who perform a certain rite or believe in a certain doctrine; for persons who eat of the same food together, according to primitive thought, acquire the same characteristics and enter into a sacramental communion. But Vedic literature does not yet show that to take food from an inferior caste was forbidden as destroying purity.<sup>85</sup> Nor, of course, has the caste system developed the constitution with a head, a council, and common festivals which the modern caste has; for such an organization is not found even in the Epic or in the P─Бli literature.<sup>86</sup> The Vedic characteristics of caste are heredity, pursuit of a common occupation, and restriction on intermarriage. <dvsn n='P'/>3. <i>Restrictions on Intermarriage.</i> тАФ Arrian, in his <i>Indica,</i><sup>87</sup> probably on the authority of Megasthenes, makes the prohibition of marriage between <lang n='greek'>╬│╬н╬╜╬╖</lang>, no doubt тАШcastes,тАЩ a characteristic of Indian life. The evidence of P─Бli literature<sup>88</sup> is in favour of this view, though it shows that a king could marry whom he wished, and could make his son by that wife the heir apparent. But it equally shows that there were others who held that not the father's but the mother's rank determined the social standing of the son. Though Manu<sup>89</sup> recognizes the possibility of marriage with the next lower caste as producing legitimate children, still he condemns the marriage of an ─Аryan with a woman of lower caste. The P─Бraskara Gс╣Ыhya S┼лtra<sup>90</sup> allows the marriage of a Kс╣гatriya with a wife of his own caste or of the lower caste, of a Brahmin with a wife of his own caste or of the two lower classes, and of a Vai┼Ыya with a Vai┼Ыya wife only. But it quotes the opinion of others that all of them can marry a ┼Ъ┼лdra wife, while other authorities condemn the marriage with a ┼Ъ┼лdra wife in certain circumstances, which implies that in other cases it might be justified.<sup>91</sup> The earlier literature bears out this impression: much stress is laid on descent from a с╣Ъс╣гi, and on purity of descent;<sup>92</sup> but there is other evidence for the view that even a Br─Бhmaс╣Зa need not be of pure lineage. <b>Kavaс╣гa</b> Ail┼лс╣гa is taunted with being the son of a D─Бs─л, тАШslave woman,тАЩ<sup>93</sup> and <b>Vatsa</b> was accused of being a ┼Ъ┼лdr─Б's son, but established his purity by walking unhurt through the flames of a fire ordeal.<sup>94</sup> He who is learned (<i>┼Ыu┼Ыruv─Бn</i>) is said to be a Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, descended from a с╣Ъс╣гi (<i>─Бrс╣гeya</i>), in the Taittir─лya Saс╣Гhit─Б;<sup>95</sup> and <b>Satyak─Бma,</b> son of <b>Jab─Бl─Б,</b> was accepted as a pupil by <b>H─Бridrumata</b> Gautama, though he could not name his father.<sup>96</sup> The K─Бс╣нhaka Saс╣Гhit─Б<sup>97</sup> says that knowledge is all-important, not descent. But all this merely goes to show that there was a measure of laxity in the hereditary character of caste, not that it was not based on heredity. The Yajurveda Saс╣Гhit─Бs<sup>98</sup> recognize the illicit union of ─Аrya and ┼Ъ┼лdr─Б, and <i>vice versa:</i> it is not unlikely that if illicit unions took place, legal marriage was quite possible. The Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>99</sup> indeed, recognizes such a case in that of <b>D─лrghatamas,</b> son of the slave girl U┼Ыij, if we may adopt the description of U┼Ыij given in the Bс╣Ыhaddevat─Б.<sup>100</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>In a hymn of the Atharvaveda<sup>101</sup> extreme claims are put forward for the Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, who alone is a true husband and the real husband, even if the woman has had others, a R─Бjanya or a Vai┼Ыya: a ┼Ъ┼лdra husband is not mentioned, probably on purpose.<sup>102</sup> The marriage of Br─Бhmaс╣Зas with R─Бjanya women is illustrated by the cases of <b>Sukany─Б,</b> daughter of king <b>┼Ъary─Бta,</b> who married <b>Cyavana,</b><sup>103</sup> and of <b>Rathav─лti's</b> daughter, who married <b>┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva.</b><sup>104</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>4. <i>Occupation and Caste.</i> тАФ The Greek authorities<sup>105</sup> and the evidence of the J─Бtakas<sup>106</sup> concur in showing it to have been the general rule that each caste was confined to its own occupations, but that the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas did engage in many professions beside that of simple priest, while all castes gave members to the ┼Ъramaс╣Зas, or homeless ascetics. The J─Бtakas<sup>107</sup> recognize the Brahmins as engaged in all sorts of occupations, as merchants, traders, agriculturists, and so forth. Matters are somewhat simpler in Vedic literature, where the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas and Kс╣гatriyas appear as practically confined to their own professions of sacrifice and military or administrative functions. Ludwig<sup>108</sup> sees in <b>D─лrgha┼Ыravas</b> in the Rigveda<sup>109</sup> a Brahmin reduced by indigence to acting as a merchant, as allowed even later by the S┼лtra literature; but this is not certain, though it is perfectly possible. More interesting is the question how far the Kс╣гatriyas practised the duties of priests; the evidence here is conflicting. The best known case is, of course, that of <b>Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra.</b> In the Rigveda he appears merely as a priest who is attached to the court of <b>Sud─Бs,</b> king of the <b>Tс╣Ыtsus;</b> but in the Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>110</sup> he is called a king, a descendant of <b>Jahnu,</b> and the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>111</sup> refers to <b>┼Ъunaс╕е┼Ыepa's</b> succeeding, through his adoption by Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra, to the divine lore (<i>daiva veda</i>) of the <b>G─Бthins</b> and the lordship of the Jahnus. That in fact this tradition is correct seems most improbable, but it serves at least to illustrate the existence of seers of royal origin. Such figures appear more than once in the Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>112</sup> which knows the technical terms <b>R─Бjanyarс╣гi</b> and <b>Devar─Бjan</b> corresponding to the later R─Бjarс╣гi, тАШroyal sage.тАЩ The Jaimin─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>113</sup> says of one who knows a certain doctrine, тАШbeing a king he becomes a seerтАЩ (<i>r─Бj─Б sann с╣Ыс╣гir</i> <i>bhavati</i>), and the Jaimin─лya Upaniс╣гad Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>114</sup> applies the term R─Бjanya to a Br─Бhmaс╣Зa. Again, it is argued that <b>Dev─Бpi</b> <b>─Аrс╣гс╣нiс╣гeс╣Зa,</b> who acted as Purohita, according to the Rigveda,<sup>115</sup> for <b>┼Ъantanu,</b> was a prince, as Y─Бska<sup>116</sup> says or implies he was.<sup>117</sup> But this assumption seems to be only an error of Y─Бska's. Since nothing in the Rigveda alludes to any relationship, it is impossible to accept Sieg's view<sup>118</sup> that the Rigveda recognizes the two as brothers, but presents the fact of a prince acting the part of Purohita as unusual and requiring explanation. The principle, however, thus accepted by Sieg as to princes in the Rigveda seems sound enough. Again, Muir<sup>119</sup> has argued that Hindu tradition, as shown in S─Бyaс╣Зa,<sup>120</sup> regards many hymns of the Rigveda as composed by royal personages, but he admits that in many cases the ascription is wrong; it may be added that in the case of <b>Pс╣Ыth─л Vainya,</b> where the hymn<sup>121</sup> ascribed to him seems to be his, it is not shown in the hymn itself that he is other than a seer; the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>122</sup> calls him a king, but that is probably of no more value than the later tradition as to Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra. The case of <b>Vi┼Ыvantara</b> and the <b>┼Ъy─Бparс╣Зas</b> mentioned in the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>123</sup> has been cited<sup>124</sup> as that of a king sacrificing without priestly aid, but the interpretation is quite uncertain, while the parallel of the <b>Ka┼Ыyapas, Asitamс╣Ыgas,</b> and <b>Bh┼лtav─лras</b> mentioned in the course of the narrative renders it highly probable that the king had other priests to carry out the sacrifice. <dvsn n='P'/>Somewhat different are a series of other cases found in the Upaniс╣гads, where the Brahma doctrine is ascribed to royal persons. Thus <b>Janaka</b> is said in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>125</sup> to have become a Brahman; <b>Aj─Бta┼Ыatru</b> taught <b>G─Бrgya</b> <b>B─Бl─Бki;</b><sup>126</sup> <b>Prav─Бhaс╣Зa Jaivali</b> instructed <b>┼Ъvetaketu ─Аruс╣Зeya,</b><sup>127</sup> as well as <b>┼Ъilaka ┼Ъ─Бl─Бvatya</b><sup>128</sup> and <b>Caikit─Бyana D─Бlbhya;</b> and <b>A┼Ыvapati Kaikeya</b> taught Brahmins.<sup>129</sup> It has been deduced<sup>130</sup> from such passages that the Brahma doctrine was a product of the Kс╣гatriyas. This conclusion is, however, entirely doubtful,<sup>131</sup> for kings were naturally willing to be flattered by the ascription to them of philosophic activity, and elsewhere<sup>132</sup> the opinion of a R─Бjanya is treated with contempt. <dvsn n='P'/>It is probably a fair deduction that the royal caste did not much concern itself with the sacred lore of the priests, though it is not unlikely that individual exceptions occurred. But that warriors became priests, that an actual change of caste took place, is quite unproved by a single genuine example. That it was impossible we cannot say, but it seems not to have taken place. To be distinguished from a caste change, as Fick<sup>133</sup> points out, is the fact that a member of any caste could, in the later period at least, become a ┼Ъramaс╣Зa, as is recorded in effect of many kings in the Epic.<sup>134</sup> Whether the practice is Vedic is not clear: Y─Бska<sup>135</sup> records it of Dev─Бpi, but this is not evidence for times much anterior to the rise of Buddhism. <dvsn n='P'/>On the other hand, the Brahmins, or at least the Purohitas, accompanied the princes in battle, and probably, like the medi├жval clergy, were not unprepared to fight,<sup>136</sup> as Vasiс╣гс╣нha and Vi┼Ыv─Бmitra seem to have done, and as priests do even in the Epic from time to time.<sup>137</sup> But a priest cannot be said to change caste by acting in this way. <dvsn n='P'/>More generally the possibility of the occurrence of change of caste may be seen in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>138</sup> where <b>┼Ъy─Бparс╣Зa S─Бyak─Бyana</b> is represented as speaking of his offspring as if they could have become the nobles, priests, and commons of the ┼Ъalvas; and in the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>139</sup> where Vi┼Ыvantara is told that if the wrong offering were made his children would be of the three other castes. A drunken с╣Ъс╣гi of the Rigveda<sup>140</sup> talks as if he could be converted into a king. On the other hand, certain kings, such as <b>Para ─Ас╣нс╣З─Бra,</b> are spoken of as performers of Sattras, тАШsacrificial sessions.тАЩ<sup>141</sup> As evidence for caste exchange all this amounts to little; later a Brahmin might become a king, while the с╣Ъс╣гi in the Rigveda is represented as speaking in a state of intoxication; the great kings could be called sacrificers if, for the nonce, they were consecrated (<i>d─лkс╣гita</i>), and so temporarily became Brahmins.<sup>142</sup> The hypothetical passages, too, do not help much. It would be unwise to deny the possibility of caste exchange, but it is not clearly indicated by any record. Even cases like that of Satyak─Бma J─Бb─Бla do not go far; for <i>ex hypothesi</i> that teacher did not know who his father was, and the latter could quite well have been a Brahmin. <dvsn n='P'/>It may therefore be held that the priests and the nobles practised hereditary occupations, and that either class was a closed body into which a man must be born. These two Varс╣Зas may thus be fairly regarded as castes. The Vai┼Ыyas offer more difficulty, for they practised a great variety of occupations (see <b>Vai┼Ыya</b>). Fick<sup>143</sup> concludes that there is no exact sense in which they can be called a caste, since, in the Buddhist literature, they were divided into various groups, which themselves practised endogamy such as the <i>gahapatis,</i> or smaller landowners, the <i>setthis,</i> or large merchants and members of the various guilds, while there are clear traces<sup>144</sup> in the legal textbooks of a view that Br─Бhmaс╣Зa and Kс╣гatriya stand opposed to all the other members of the community. But we need hardly accept this view for Vedic times, when the Vai┼Ыya, the ordinary freeman of the tribe, formed a class or caste in all probability, which was severed by its free status from the ┼Ъ┼лdras, and which was severed by its lack of priestly or noble blood from the two higher classes in the state. It is probably legitimate to hold that any Vai┼Ыya could marry any member of the caste, and that the later divisions within the category of Vai┼Ыyas are growths of divisions parallel with the original process by which priest and noble had grown into separate entities. The process can be seen to day when new tribes fall under the caste system: each class tries to elevate itself in the social scale by refusing to intermarry with inferior classes on equal terms тАФ hypergamy is often allowed тАФ and so those Vai┼Ыyas who acquired wealth in trade (<b>┼Ъreс╣гс╣нhin</b>) or agriculture (the P─Бli Gahapatis) would become distinct, as sub-castes, from the ordinary Vai┼Ыyas. But it is not legitimate to regard Vai┼Ыya as a theoretic caste; rather it is an old caste which is in process of dividing into innumerable sub-castes under influences of occupation, religion, or geographical situation. <dvsn n='P'/>Fick<sup>145</sup> denies also that the ┼Ъ┼лdras ever formed a single caste: he regards the term as covering the numerous inferior races and tribes defeated by the ─Аryan invaders, but originally as denoting only one special tribe. It is reasonable to suppose that ┼Ъ┼лdra was the name given by the Vedic Indians to the nations opposing them, and that these ranked as slaves beside the three castes тАФ nobles, priests, and people тАФ just as in the Anglo-Saxon and early German constitution beside the priests, the <i>nobiles</i> or eorls, and the <i>ingenui,</i> ordinary freemen or ceorls, there was a distinct class of slaves proper; the use of a generic expression to cover them seems natural, whatever its origin (see <b>┼Ъ┼лdra</b>). In the ─Аryan view a marriage of ┼Ъ┼лdras could hardly be regulated by rules; any ┼Ъ┼лdra could wed another, if such a marriage could be called a marriage at all, for a slave cannot in early law be deemed to be capable of marriage proper. But what applied in the early Vedic period became no doubt less and less applicable later when many aboriginal tribes and princes must have come into the ─Аryan community by peaceful means, or by conquest, without loss of personal liberty, and when the term ┼Ъ┼лdra would cover many sorts of people who were not really slaves, but were freemen of a humble character occupied in such functions as supplying the numerous needs of the village, like the <b>Caс╣Зс╕Н─Бlas,</b> or tribes living under ─Аryan control, or independent, such as the <b>Niс╣г─Бdas.</b> <dvsn n='P'/>But it is also probable that the ┼Ъ┼лdras came to include men of ─Аryan race, and that the Vedic period saw the degradation of ─Аryans to a lower social status. This seems, at any rate, to have been the case with the <b>Rathak─Бras.</b> In the Taittir─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa<sup>146</sup> the Rathak─Бra is placed as a special class along with the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas, R─Бjanyas, and Vai┼Ыyas: this can hardly be interpreted except to mean that the Rathak─Бras were not included in the ─Аryan classes, though it is just possible that only a subdivision of the Vai┼Ыyas is meant. There is other evidence<sup>147</sup> that the Rathak─Бras were regarded as ┼Ъ┼лdras. But in the Atharvaveda<sup>148</sup> the Rathak─Бras and the <b>Karm─Бras</b> appear in a position of importance in connexion with the selection of the king; these two classes are also referred to in an honourable way in the V─Бjasaneyi Saс╣Гhit─Б;<sup>149</sup> in the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>150</sup> too, the Rathak─Бra is mentioned as a a person of high standing. It is impossible to accept the view suggested by Fick<sup>151</sup> that these classes were originally non─Аryan; we must recognize that the Rathak─Бras, in early Vedic times esteemed for their skill, later became degraded because of the growth of the feeling that manual labour was not dignified. The development of this idea was a departure from the ─Аryan conception; it is not unnatural, however undesirable, and has a faint parallel in the class distinctions of modern Europe. Similarly, the Karm─Бra, the <b>Takс╣гan,</b><sup>152</sup> the Carmamna, or тАШtanner,тАЩ the weaver and others, quite dignified occupations in the Rigveda, are reckoned as ┼Ъ┼лdras in the P─Бli texts.<sup>153</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>The later theory, which appears fully developed in the Dharma S┼лtras,<sup>154</sup> deduces the several castes other than the original four from the intermarriage of the several castes. This theory has no justification in the early Vedic literature. In some cases it is obviously wrong; for example, the <b>S┼лta</b> is said to be a caste of this kind, whereas it is perfectly clear that if the S┼лtas did form a caste, it was one ultimately due to occupation. But there is no evidence at all that the S┼лtas, Gr─Бmaс╣З─лs, and other members of occupations were real castes in the sense that they were endogamic in the early Vedic period. All that we can say is that there was a steady progress by which caste after caste was formed, occupation being an important determining feature, just as in modern times there are castes bearing names like Gop─Бla (тАШcowherdтАЩ) Kaivarta or Dh─лvara (тАШfishermanтАЩ), and Vaс╣Зij (тАШmerchantтАЩ).<sup>155</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>Fick<sup>156</sup> finds in the J─Бtakas mention of a number of occupations whose members did not form part of any caste at all, such as the attendants on the court, the actors and dancers who went from village to village, and the wild tribes that lived in the mountains, fishermen, hunters, and so on. In Vedic times these people presumably fell under the conception of ┼Ъ┼лdra, and may have included the <b>Parс╣Зaka, Paulkasa, Bainda,</b> who are mentioned with many others in the V─Бjasaneyi Saс╣Гhit─Б and the Taittir─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa in the list of victims at the Puruс╣гamedha (тАШhuman sacrificeтАЩ). The slaves also, whom Fick<sup>157</sup> includes in the same category, were certainly included in the term ┼Ъ┼лdra. <dvsn n='P'/>5. <i>Origin of the Castes.</i> тАФ The question of the origin of the castes presents some difficulty. The ultimate cause of the extreme rigidity of the caste system, as compared with the features of any other ─Аryan society, must probably be sought in the sharp distinction drawn from the beginning between the ─Аryan and the ┼Ъ┼лdra. The contrast which the Vedic Indians felt as existing between themselves and the conquered population, and which probably rested originally on the difference of colour between the upper and the lower classes, tended to accentuate the natural distinctions of birth, occupation, and locality which normally existed among the ─Аryan Indians, but which among other ─Аryan peoples never developed into a caste system like that of India. The doctrine of hypergamy which marks the practical working of the caste system, seems clearly to point to the feeling that the ─Аryan could marry the ┼Ъ┼лdr─Б, but not the ┼Ъ┼лdra the ─Аry─Б. This distinction probably lies at the back of all other divisions: its force may be illustrated by the peculiar state of feeling as to mixed marriages, for example, in the Southern States of America and in South Africa, or even in India itself, between the new invaders from Europe and the mingled population which now peoples the country. Marriages between persons of the white and the dark race are disapproved in principle, but varying degrees of condemnation attach to (1) the marriage of a man of the white race with a woman of the dark race; (2) an informal connexion between these two; (3) a marriage between a woman of the white race and a man of the dark race; and (4) an informal connexion between these two. Each category, on the whole, is subject to more severe reprobation than the preceding one. This race element, it would seem, is what has converted social divisions into castes. There appears, then, to be a large element of truth in the theory, best represented by Risley,<sup>158</sup> which explains caste in the main as a matter of blood, and which holds that the higher the caste is, the greater is the proportion of ─Аryan blood. <dvsn n='P'/>The chief rival theory is undoubtedly that of Senart,<sup>159</sup> which places the greatest stress on the ─Аryan constitution of the family. According to Senart the ─Аryan people practised in affairs of marriage both a rule of exogamy, and one of endogamy. A man must marry a woman of equal birth, but not one of the same <i>gens,</i> according to Roman law as interpreted by Senart and Kovalevsky;<sup>160</sup> and an Athenian must marry an Athenian woman, but not one of the same <lang n='greek'>╬│╬н╬╜╬┐╧В</lang>. In India these rules are reproduced in the form that one must not marry within the <b>Gotra,</b> but not without the caste. The theory, though attractively developed, is not convincing; the Latin and Greek parallels are not even probably accurate;<sup>161</sup> and in India the rule forbidding marriage within the Gotra is one which grows; in strictness as the evidence grows later in date.<sup>162</sup> <dvsn n='P'/>On the other hand, it is not necessary to deny that the development of caste may have been helped by the family traditions of some <i>gentes,</i> or <lang n='greek'>╬│╬н╬╜╬╖</lang>, or Gotras. The Patricians of Rome for a long time declined intermarriage with the plebeians; the Athenian Eupatridai seem to have kept their <lang n='greek'>╬│╬н╬╜╬╖</lang> pure from contamination by union with lower blood; and there may well have been noble families among the Vedic Indians who intermarried only among themselves. The Germans known to Tacitus<sup>163</sup> were divided into <i>nobiles</i> and <i>ingenui,</i> and the Anglo-Saxons into eorls and ceorls, noble and non-noble freemen.<sup>164</sup> The origin of nobility need not be sought in the Vedic period proper, for it may already have existed. It may have been due to the fact that the king, whom we must regard as originally elected by the people, was as king often in close relation with, or regarded as an incarnation of, the deity;<sup>165</sup> and that hereditary kingship would tend to increase the tradition of especially sacred blood: thus the royal family and its offshoots would be anxious to maintain the purity of their blood. In India, beside the sanctity of the king, there was the sanctity of the priest. Here we have in the family exclusiveness of king and nobles, and the similar exclusiveness of a priesthood which was not celibate, influences that make for caste, especially when accompanying the deep opposition between the general folk and the servile aborigines. <dvsn n='P'/>Caste, once created, naturally developed in different directions. Nesfield<sup>166</sup> was inclined to see in occupation the one ground of caste. It is hardly necessary seriously to criticize this view considered as an ultimate explanation of caste, but it is perfectly certain that gilds of workers tend to become castes. The carpenters (<b>Takс╣гan</b>), the chariot-makers (<b>Rathak─Бra</b>), the fishermen (<b>Dhaivara</b>) and others are clearly of the type of caste, and the number extends itself as time goes on. But this is not to say that caste is founded on occupation pure and simple in its first origin, or that mere difference of occupation would have produced the system of caste without the interposition of the fundamental difference between ─Аryan and D─Бsa or ┼Ъ┼лdra blood and colour. This difference rendered increasingly important what the history of the ─Аryan peoples shows us to be declining, the distinction between the noble and the non-noble freemen, a distinction not of course ultimate, but one which seems to have been developed in the ─Аryan people before the separation of its various branches. <dvsn n='P'/>It is well known that the Iranian polity presents a division of classes comparable in some respects<sup>167</sup> with the Indian polity. The priests (Atharvas) and warriors (Rathaesthas) are unmistakably parallel, and the two lower classes seem to correspond closely to the P─Бli Gahapatis, and perhaps to the ┼Ъ┼лdras.<sup>168</sup> But they are certainly not castes in the Indian sense of the word. There is no probability in the view of Senart<sup>169</sup> or of Risley<sup>170</sup> that the names of the old classes were later superimposed artificially on a system of castes that were different from them in origin. We cannot say that the castes existed before the classes, and that the classes were borrowed by India from Iran, as Risley maintains, ignoring the early Br─Бhmaс╣Зa evidence for the four Varс╣Зas, and treating the transfer as late. Nor can we say with Senart that the castes and classes are of independent origin. If there had been no Varс╣Зa, caste might never have arisen; both colour and class occupation are needed for a plausible account of the rise of caste.<sup>171</sup> <F>1) <i>D─Бsa,</i> Rv. ii. 12, 4; <i>─Бrya varс╣Зa</i> as against <i>dasyu,</i> iii. 34, 9; <i>varс╣Зa</i> itself opposed to <i>d─Бsa,</i> i. 104, 2. <i>Cf.</i> ii. 3, 5. <i>Cf.</i> a verse in ┼Ъ─Бс╣Еkh─Бyana ┼Ъrauta S┼лtra, viii. 25, 2; Pa├▒caviс╣Г┼Ыa Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, v. 5, 14. Senart, <i>Les Castes dans</i> <i>l'Inde,</i> 48 <i>et seq.,</i> 212 <i>et seq.,</i> attributes great importance to the question of eating together, and compares the sacrificial meals of the <i>gens</i> at Rome, where strangers were excluded (Fustel de Coulanges, <i>La Cit├й Antique,</i> 117). But this is not conclusive; a caste is not a <i>gens,</i> and the <i>gens</i> excluded strangers only at a solemn festival, when the whole <i>gens</i> renewed its blood kinship. If we have no evidence exactly establishing this for the Gotra in early Vedic literature, we need not hesitate to believe that in the earliest Vedic period the Gotra had solemn festivals of union, and of communication with the dead, but that again does not explain or amount to the caste prohibition of taking food from an inferior.</F> <F>84) <i>E.g.,</i> Aitareya ─Аraс╣Зyaka, v. 3, 3. with Keith's note.</F> <F>85) For a case of objection to eating food after another, see Ch─Бndogya Upaniс╣гad, i. 10, 1.
